And from that moment on, nothing will ever be the same…</span>

<div class="communityQuestion">
<div class="questionText">
<a href="/questions/118136-what-was-the-point-of-sam-black-crow">What was the point (?) of Sam Black Crow? Why did she dream of the buffalo headed person, and the thunderbirds? Why was she given so much significance at the end of the book, when she hadn't really been in the rest of it very much? And how did she get the flowers from Shadow???</a>
</div>

<div class="answererUserIcon">
<a href="/user/show/7455082-vintage274" class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" alt="" /></a>
</div>
<div class="communityAnswerTextContainer">
<span class="communityAnswererName">
<a href="/user/show/7455082-vintage274">Vintage274</a>
</span>
<span class="communityAnswerText">
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Sam says herself that she dreams she was a shaman with a shriveled arm in her past life. She is Atsula the holy woman of the matriarchal tribe that<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Sam says herself that she dreams she was a shaman with a shriveled arm in her past life. She is Atsula the holy woman of the matriarchal tribe that emigrates across the Bering Strait to America and is sacrificed at the foot of the mountain. Shadow cannot be seen when he gives her the flowers. He's a "shadowy" observer.<a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div class="allCommunityAnswers">
<div class="communityAnswer">
<div class="communityAnswerBody">
<div class="answererUserIcon">
<a href="/user/show/11196919-alex" class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto"><img src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/users/1428997206i/11196919._UY100_CR7,0,100,100_.jpg" alt="" /></a>
</div>
<div class="communityAnswerTextContainer">
<span class="communityAnswererName">
<a href="/user/show/11196919-alex">Alex</a>
</span>
<span class="communityAnswerText">
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Short answer: Redemption and closure.<br /><br />Long answer: Throughout the novel, there was a theme of redemption and paying your debts. Laura committed the<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Short answer: Redemption and closure.<br /><br />Long answer: Throughout the novel, there was a theme of redemption and paying your debts. Laura committed the moral crime of infidelity while Shadow was incarcerated. You could say she was given the ultimate punishment for committing her sin.<br /><br />As a reanimated being, she was given a second chance at making it up to Shadow. She gained redemption by assisting Shadow in his journey. When she killed Loki/Mr. World, she completed her own journey of redemption. Now that her purpose has been fulfilled, Shadow forgave her and allowed her to go back to resting in peace, permanently.<a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_24033270">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24033270" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1223383-david-monroe" class="left imgcol" title="David Monroe"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1329255593p2/1223383.jpg" alt="David Monroe" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/24033270?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 28, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1223383-david-monroe" class="user" title="David Monroe">David Monroe</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/11275360-american-gods" title="ebook 9780062109590" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1223383-david-monroe?shelf=book-to-re-read" class="actionLinkLite">book-to-re-read</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1223383-david-monroe?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1223383-david-monroe?shelf=urban-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1223383-david-monroe?shelf=faves" class="actionLinkLite">faves</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer24033270">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9323585166253385579">Anybody who tells you that the book is about old and new gods, or about a man named Shadow, or about coin tricks, or about having one's head smashed in for losing a game of checkers, is selling you a line, because those are just details, not the story itself.<br /><br />Much like any Neil Gaiman story, the devil is in the details, and you just have to resolve yourself to coming along for the ride or you'll miss it. It's not one story, or two, it's many, and it's all complete...and you have to just read it,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9323585166253385579">Anybody who tells you that the book is about old and new gods, or about a man named Shadow, or about coin tricks, or about having one's head smashed in for losing a game of checkers, is selling you a line, because those are just details, not the story itself.<br /><br />Much like any Neil Gaiman story, the devil is in the details, and you just have to resolve yourself to coming along for the ride or you'll miss it. It's not one story, or two, it's many, and it's all complete...and you have to just read it, and enjoy it, and accept it. Or just don't bother.<br /><br />I might as well sell you a violin as sell this book to you, or pluck a synopsis of it from behind your ear and then deposit it in my hand, only to have it turn into a critical review while your attention is elsewhere. But I won't, you'll just have to find the magic yourself.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9323585166253385579">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_18557532">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18557532" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1020736-stacey" class="left imgcol" title="Stacey"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1391972803p2/1020736.jpg" alt="Stacey" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/18557532?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 05, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1020736-stacey" class="user" title="Stacey">Stacey</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/567724.American_Gods" title="Hardcover 9780380973651" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1020736-stacey?shelf=all-time-favorites" class="actionLinkLite">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1020736-stacey?shelf=audio" class="actionLinkLite">audio</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1020736-stacey?shelf=myths-and-fairy-tales" class="actionLinkLite">myths-and-fairy-tales</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1020736-stacey?shelf=my-library-hardcover" class="actionLinkLite">my-library-hardcover</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1020736-stacey?shelf=signed" class="actionLinkLite">signed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1020736-stacey?shelf=my-master-list-of-sci-fi" class="actionLinkLite">my-master-list-of-sci-fi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1020736-stacey?shelf=imo" class="actionLinkLite">imo</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer18557532">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1828348977926226060">In 2003, I walked away from my childhood religion – a high control (some would say abusive) group with a tiny little worldview and a severe superiority complex. <br /><br />This was my reality:<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380309125i/688206.jpg" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380309125i/688207.jpg" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380309125i/688208.jpg" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380309125i/688209.jpg" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380309125i/688210.jpg" /><br /><br />I believed with all my being that the things depicted above were real, and were just over the event horizon. <br /><br />Leaving meant losing almost every friend I had ever made since childhood, it created a rift with my still devout family, and quite possibly saved my life. <br /><br />Is it any wonder that fiction – alternate realities, fa</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1828348977926226060">In 2003, I walked away from my childhood religion – a high control (some would say abusive) group with a tiny little worldview and a severe superiority complex. <br /><br />This was my reality:<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380309125i/688206.jpg" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380309125i/688207.jpg" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380309125i/688208.jpg" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380309125i/688209.jpg" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380309125i/688210.jpg" /><br /><br />I believed with all my being that the things depicted above were real, and were just over the event horizon. <br /><br />Leaving meant losing almost every friend I had ever made since childhood, it created a rift with my still devout family, and quite possibly saved my life. <br /><br />Is it any wonder that fiction – alternate realities, fantasy, and mental escape – helped me make that decision, helped me move on, and helped deprogram my cult-think? One fiction supplanted the other, only this time I <em>already knew</em> I was working with stories. <br /><br />Some of this fiction I had read many times, not understanding why the stories resonated so strongly within me, just knowing that I was compelled to return to those worlds, over and over. Others were stories I read during the time surrounding my breakaway, and shortly thereafter.*<br /><br /><em>American Gods</em> made me observe and think differently. It gave me a new context for the mythologies I had accepted for most of my life. It was bigger than the story of Shadow, or the girl Sam, or Czernabog. For me, it was about how we allow our Old Gods to define our present worldview, and how we allow our New Gods to steal our awareness. Our mythologies set the boundaries of our culture, and paradoxically, as our culture changes, our gods sacrifice their immortality. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>"Religions are, by definition, metaphors, after all: God is a dream, a hope, a woman, an ironist, a father, a city, a house of many rooms, a watchmaker who left his prize chronometer in the desert, someone who loves you--even, perhaps, against all evidence, a celestial being whose only interest is to make sure your football team, army, business, or marriage thrives, prospers, and triumphs over all opposition."</em>
</blockquote><br /><br />The part of the story that affected me the most profoundly was the story of Hinzelmann and Lakeside. The mixing of good and evil, the blurring of lines, townspeople looking the other way – to such a degree that it never occurs to them to see what is happening right under their noses. Dead men's bones. Deaths of legends. It affected me to my core. During the time I was reading <em>American Gods</em>, it was <em>this</em> which rocked me – I was doing the same thing – choosing and keeping and killing my own Gods, my own mythologies. <br /><br />It was tremendously painful, made a little easier by having the opportunity to process it within the bounds of somebody else's story.<br /><br /><br />*The rest of the list:<br /><br /><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/234225.Dune">Dune</a><br /><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/105.Chapterhouse">Chapterhouse Dune</a><br /><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/119787.Fahrenheit_451">Fahrenheit 451</a><br /><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7613.Animal_Farm">Animal Farm</a><br /><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5470.1984">1984</a><br /><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/582562.The_Absolute_Sandman_Vol_1">Sandman</a><br /><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1310174.Crisis_of_Conscience">Crisis of Conscience</a><br /><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10847.Under_the_Banner_of_Heaven">Under the Banner of Heaven</a><br /><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/345220.Seductive_Poison">Seductive Poison</a><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1828348977926226060">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_102911371">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/102911371" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya" class="left imgcol" title="Nataliya"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1395089173p2/3672777.jpg" alt="Nataliya" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/102911371?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 04, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya" class="user" title="Nataliya">Nataliya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/983100.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780060558123" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=excellent-reads" class="actionLinkLite">excellent-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2011-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2011-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=hugo-nebula" class="actionLinkLite">hugo-nebula</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=locus-winner" class="actionLinkLite">locus-winner</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer102911371">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3926372794249372404">Neil Gaiman must have British gonads of titanium to write a huge sprawling epic story about the nature of American belief. It's a gamble that worked perfectly - since, as he said, <em>"Nobody's American [...] Not originally. That's my point."</em><br /><br /><img width="400" height="130" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380320723i/689953._SX540_.jpg" /><p><br /><em>"<strong>It's a god-eat-god world.</strong>"</em> This quote by Sir Terry Pratchett, another amazing British writer, perfectly summarizes the surface plot of the intimidating bulk of <em>American Gods</em>.</p><blockquote>"<em>It's what people do. They believe, and then they do not take responsibility for their</em></blockquote></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3926372794249372404">Neil Gaiman must have British gonads of titanium to write a huge sprawling epic story about the nature of American belief. It's a gamble that worked perfectly - since, as he said, <em>"Nobody's American [...] Not originally. That's my point."</em><br /><br /><img width="400" height="130" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380320723i/689953._SX540_.jpg" /><p><br /><em>"<strong>It's a god-eat-god world.</strong>"</em> This quote by Sir Terry Pratchett, another amazing British writer, perfectly summarizes the surface plot of the intimidating bulk of <em>American Gods</em>.</p><blockquote>"<em>It's what people do. They believe, and then they do not take responsibility for their beliefs; they conjure things, and do not trust the conjuration. People populate the darkness; with ghosts, with gods, with electrons, with tales. People imagine, and people believe; and it is that rock solid belief, that makes things happen. </em>"</blockquote><strong><em>American Gods</em> is Gaiman's ode to America,</strong> the land which has become a melting pot to more than just people. It also took in the beliefs they willingly or not brought with them to the New World, embraced them, changed them, allowed some to flourish and others to nearly wither away. It pitted the old gods not only against each other in the endless battle for survival, but also against the new deities of consumerism and technology for the precious belief that keeps them going. <strong>To quote Sir Pratchett again, <em>"You need to believe in things that aren't true. How else can they become?"</em>.</strong><blockquote>"<em>Gods die. And when they truly die they are unmourned and unremembered. Ideas are more difficult to kill than people, but they can be killed, in the end.</em>"</blockquote><img width="400" height="130" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380320723i/689954._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />But the squabble among the old and new gods is just the surface. The heart of Gaiman's novel lies in portrayal - through seemingly unconnected interludes and sketches - of the people who brought the legends to this land, brought them among blood and loss and sorrow and heartbreak, of the people now who live in the this patchwork country that made a whole out of many little bits, of their origins and pasts, and of the soul of present-day America, the glue that holds it together.<blockquote>"<em>There was a girl, and her uncle sold her. Put like that it seems so simple.</em>"</blockquote>And to top it all off, we are treated to the portrait of a seemingly idyllic, very Stephen King-esque small American town of Lakeside, hiding its own dark deep secret. Lakeview, the quintessential American small town, the stuff of legends, as one may say. Terrifying legends, indeed.<br /><br />Don't be fooled into thinking the ex-con Shadow working for a mysterious Mr. Wednesday (whose real identity is not that hard to spot almost immediately) is the protagonist. No, Shadow feels flat and underdeveloped simply because <strong>he is just our binoculars into the vast landscape of American mythology, this world of belief and legends. </strong><blockquote>"<em>What should I believe? thought Shadow, and the voice came back to him from somewhere deep beneath the world, in a bass rumble: Believe everything.</em>"</blockquote><strong>The imagery that Gaiman creates is stunning.</strong> He paints a vivid picture with confident brush strokes, creating an unforgettable literary landscape. And he takes a gamble with the storyline and the plotting as well. Do not look for exciting battles and confrontations, for non-stop action or fast-moving plot. This is the book unfolding slowly and finding its depth in the side stories and interludes that are there not to move the plot forward in the traditional sense but to give an extra glimpse, an extra dimension to the unfolding epic picture.<br /><br /><strong>5 stars for the unforgettable fascinating fully-immersing experience. </strong>Mr. Gaiman, if you want a title of an honorary American from this awestruck immigrant, well - here it is.<blockquote>"<em>What I say is, a town isn't a town without a bookstore. It may call itself a town, but unless it's got a bookstore it knows it's not fooling a soul.</em>"</blockquote></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3926372794249372404">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_229605390">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/229605390" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5691412-meg" class="left imgcol" title="Meg ?"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1356897271p2/5691412.jpg" alt="Meg ?" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/229605390?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 24, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5691412-meg" class="user" title="Meg ?">Meg ?</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/9464129-american-gods" title="Hardcover 9780062059888" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5691412-meg?shelf=e-books" class="actionLinkLite">e-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5691412-meg?shelf=z-2012-reads" class="actionLinkLite">z-2012-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5691412-meg?shelf=disappointing" class="actionLinkLite">disappointing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer229605390">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11171121791351511344">This is a tough review for me to write. I'm not exactly sure what it is about this book that I don't like. I'm not sure there even IS something I don't like. Since I don't want to just leave you all with the ever popular "I'm just not that into it", I will try to explain.<br /><br />This book has all the elements of a book I would enjoy. The creepiness factor is up there, the writing is brilliant, the main character is a big lug I couldn't help but love. Also, I have always been fascinated by mythology, so</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11171121791351511344">This is a tough review for me to write. I'm not exactly sure what it is about this book that I don't like. I'm not sure there even IS something I don't like. Since I don't want to just leave you all with the ever popular "I'm just not that into it", I will try to explain.<br /><br />This book has all the elements of a book I would enjoy. The creepiness factor is up there, the writing is brilliant, the main character is a big lug I couldn't help but love. Also, I have always been fascinated by mythology, so that's a plus.<br /><br />Shadow is our main character and he just got out of jail after doing his time of three years. Right before he is supposed to be released he is let out early, because his wife was killed, in apparently scandalous circumstances. The first 50ish pages were about the extent of where the book was interesting to me. Shadow meets Wednesday, and then the story turns into a bunch of mini stories and flashbacks, and I didn't enjoy most of them. Some were okay, but the majority just felt like annoying disruptions, and I felt myself thinking this is yet another longer book that could benefit from losing about 100 or so pages from the dragging middle. Shadow is paid by Wednesday to be an errand boy while he travels America trying to rally his troops in preparation for a war between The old Gods, and the new Gods (media and money) I guess it's my own fault. I couldn't really bring myself to care about this war between the new and old Gods, because the Gods of Media and Money? Not my Gods...<br /><br />Books that are hyped up as much as this one leave me in a place where I tend to get disappointed, because it's so hard to live up to those expectations. Of course that's not the books fault, but I was just expecting to like this book much more than I did. I never felt engaged while reading this book, and that's the reason I couldn't rate this above three stars. I could appreciate the great writing and originality, however, so I couldn't give it below three stars.<br /><br />Three stars it is folks, but as most of you know this book is loved by (almost) all, so of course I encourage everyone who is interested in this book already to read it, and form your own opinions. This book didn't do it for me, but I am definitely going to try some of Gaiman's other books and see if I have a better experience.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11171121791351511344">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_61530162">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61530162" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/922495-patrick" class="left imgcol" title="Patrick"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1351307095p2/922495.jpg" alt="Patrick" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/61530162?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 08, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/922495-patrick" class="user" title="Patrick">Patrick</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4407.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780747263746" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer61530162">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17818802607407661306">Whenever we have a cold snap here in Wisconsin, I find myself thinking about one of my favorite pieces of American Gods. <br /><br />I remember reading it back in 2002 or so. This was back in the day. Back when it was a bit of a secret that Gaiman lived in Wisconsin. <br /><br />I read the following section of the book nodding to myself, thinking, "Yup, that's exactly what it's like." <br /><br />Then I had another thought: "I bet this comes from that really bad cold snap we had here in Wisconsin about six years ago." <br /><br />It was prett</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17818802607407661306">Whenever we have a cold snap here in Wisconsin, I find myself thinking about one of my favorite pieces of American Gods. <br /><br />I remember reading it back in 2002 or so. This was back in the day. Back when it was a bit of a secret that Gaiman lived in Wisconsin. <br /><br />I read the following section of the book nodding to myself, thinking, "Yup, that's exactly what it's like." <br /><br />Then I had another thought: "I bet this comes from that really bad cold snap we had here in Wisconsin about six years ago." <br /><br />It was pretty cool for me, being able to guess where a this piece of this book got its start.... <br /><br />For those of you who haven't read it: here's the excerpt. The main character, Shadow, has just come to a small Wisconsin town, and he decides to walk into town to buy some warmer clothes and groceries. <br /><br />* * * <br /><br />The cold snap had come, that was for sure. It could not be much above zero, and it would not be a pleasant walk, but he was certain he could make it into town without too much trouble. What did Hinzelmann say last night—a ten-minute walk? And Shadow was a big man. He would walk briskly and keep himself warm. He set off south, heading for the bridge.<br /><br />Soon he began to cough, a dry, thin cough, as the bitterly cold air touched his lungs. Soon his ears and face and lips hurt, and then his feet hurt. He thrust his ungloved hands deep into his coat pockets, clenched his fingers together trying to find some warmth. <br /><br />[...]<br /><br />Step after step after step. He glanced back. The apartment building was not as far away as he had expected.<br /><br />This walk, he decided, was a mistake. But he was already three or four minutes from the apartment, and the bridge over the lake was in sight. It made as much sense to press on as to go home (and then what? Call a taxi on the dead phone? Wait for spring? He had no food in the apartment, he reminded himself).<br /><br />He kept walking, revising his estimates of the temperature downward as he walked. Minus ten? Minus twenty? Minus forty, maybe, that strange point on the thermometer when Celsius and Fahrenheit say the same thing. Probably not that cold. But then there was wind chill, and the wind was now hard and steady and continuous, blowing over the lake, coming down from the Arctic across Canada.<br /><br />Ten more minutes of walking, he guessed, and the bridge seemed to be no nearer. He was too cold to shiver. His eyes hurt. This was not simply cold: this was science fiction. This was a story set on the dark side of Mercury, back when they thought Mercury had a dark side. This was somewhere out on rocky Pluto, where the sun is just another star, shining only a little more brightly in the darkness. This, thought Shadow, is just a hair away from the places where air comes in buckets and pours just like beer.<br /><br />The occasional cars that roared past him seemed unreal: spaceships, little freeze-dried packages of metal and glass, inhabited by people dressed more warmly than he was. An old song his mother had loved, “Walking in a Winter Wonderland,” began to run through his head, and he hummed it through closed lips, kept pace to it as he walked.<br /><br />He had lost all sensation in his feet. He looked down at his black leather shoes, at the thin cotton socks, and began, seriously, to worry about frostbite.<br /><br />This was beyond a joke. This had moved beyond foolishness, slipped over the line into genuine twenty-four-karat Jesus-Christ-I-screwed-up-big-time territory. His clothes might as well have been netting or lace: the wind blew through him, froze his bones and the marrow in his bones, froze the lashes of his eyes, froze the warm place under his balls, which were retreating into his pelvic cavity.<br /><br />Keep walking, he told himself. Keep walking. I can stop and drink a pail of air when I get home....<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />And that, my friends, is one of the many reasons I love Neil Gaiman.... <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17818802607407661306">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_26001211">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26001211" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/177596-oceana2602" class="left imgcol" title="Oceana2602"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1183922736p2/177596.jpg" alt="Oceana2602" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/26001211?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 22, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/177596-oceana2602" class="user" title="Oceana2602">Oceana2602</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/177596-oceana2602?shelf=2007" class="actionLinkLite">2007</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/177596-oceana2602?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/177596-oceana2602?shelf=english" class="actionLinkLite">english</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/177596-oceana2602?shelf=male-writers" class="actionLinkLite">male-writers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer26001211">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2930396920732922496">"Read Gaiman!" they say. "I can't believe you've never read Gaiman! You have GOT TO read Gaiman!" "Gaiman is SUCH an important part of popular culture and one of the BEST contemporary writers! You HAVE TO READ GAIMAN!"<br /><br />Well, I've read Gaiman now.<br /><br />Hi Gaiman!<br />Bye Gaiman!<br /><br /><br />Let me quote:<br /><em>"American Gods is Neil Gaiman's best and most ambitious novel yet, a scary, strange, and hallucinogenic road-trip story wrapped around a deep examination of the American spirit."</em><br /><br />I agree with everything but the beginning a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2930396920732922496">"Read Gaiman!" they say. "I can't believe you've never read Gaiman! You have GOT TO read Gaiman!" "Gaiman is SUCH an important part of popular culture and one of the BEST contemporary writers! You HAVE TO READ GAIMAN!"<br /><br />Well, I've read Gaiman now.<br /><br />Hi Gaiman!<br />Bye Gaiman!<br /><br /><br />Let me quote:<br /><em>"American Gods is Neil Gaiman's best and most ambitious novel yet, a scary, strange, and hallucinogenic road-trip story wrapped around a deep examination of the American spirit."</em><br /><br />I agree with everything but the beginning and the end. It certainly was scary, strange and hallucinogenic. <br />None of it in a good way.<br /><br />I like nothing about this book. Not liking it isn't very difficult, because I have honestly no idea what was going on. Not that I didn't get the actual story, it wasn't that hard, since Mr. Gaiman sure isn't the most demanding writer (that isn't meant as a criticism, it can be a good thing). But why the things that were going on, were going on, completely eluded me. And while I kept on reading and wondering, 'huh? why? What now?', in the end, it all came up to "Why should I care?"<br /><br />This isn't my kind of book, mainly due to the subject and the characters. That's why I don't think anything Gaiman wrote would be my kind of book. It certainly isn't a book, or an author, you HAVE to read.<br /><br />I guess this, like that strange car race video game and Star Trek, will be parts of popular culture that will have to live without me. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2930396920732922496">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_179826641">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/179826641" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1413439-stephen" class="left imgcol" title="Stephen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" alt="Stephen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/179826641?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 07, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1413439-stephen" class="user" title="Stephen">Stephen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/9464129-american-gods" title="Hardcover 9780062059888" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=6-star-books" class="actionLinkLite">6-star-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=all-time-favorites" class="actionLinkLite">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=audiobook" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=mythstories-and-legends" class="actionLinkLite">mythstories-and-legends</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=favorite-ideas" class="actionLinkLite">favorite-ideas</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=2000-2005" class="actionLinkLite">2000-2005</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer179826641">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2444766218715739590"><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Book%20covers/?action=view&amp;current=tumblr_ln5cftpZUU1qzt60oo1_r1_400-1.jpg"> <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380320723i/689955.jpg" /></a><br /><br />My literary promiscuity being what it is, I have read <u>and loved</u> a lot of novels in many different genres. However, among the beau coup books that I have <em>loved long time</em> there are a select few that hold a special, hallowed place in my pantheon of favorites…American Gods is one of these elite. <br /><br />Gaiman’s writing is both subdued and poetic. It is deeply emotional, but without a hint of melodrama. His descriptions are elaborate yet not drawn out. He tells a huge, complex, eternal story, one small tal</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2444766218715739590"><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Book%20covers/?action=view&amp;current=tumblr_ln5cftpZUU1qzt60oo1_r1_400-1.jpg">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380320723i/689955.jpg" /></a><br /><br />My literary promiscuity being what it is, I have read <u>and loved</u> a lot of novels in many different genres. However, among the beau coup books that I have <em>loved long time</em> there are a select few that hold a special, hallowed place in my pantheon of favorites…American Gods is one of these elite. <br /><br />Gaiman’s writing is both subdued and poetic. It is deeply emotional, but without a hint of melodrama. His descriptions are elaborate yet not drawn out. He tells a huge, complex, eternal story, one small tale at a time. I don’t know how else to say it, in this book Neil Gaiman took story telling and made it his bitch. <br /><br />Therefore, upon learning that I had the opportunity to read Neil Gaiman’s preferred and expanded version of AG containing an additional 12000 words, my reaction is what you might expect. I exploded into tears of ecstasy, lost my bowels and wept uncontrollably for well over an hour. This made for a particularly awkward moment at the book store but at least I was prepared…<br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Goodreads/?action=view&amp;current=UW_Men_Super_19842_L-XL_350-1.jpg">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1379765680i/239193.jpg" /></a> <br />Later, when I was calmed down, cleaned up and baby powdered, I sat down and tore through this over the weekend. <br /><br />Since this is the 10th anniversary edition of this modern fantasy classic, I am going to assume that a fair number of you reading this are at least familiar with the story. However, I will still avoid major spoilers in this review, except for disclosing (1) the identity of Wednesday and (2) the basic plot. Both of these things are revealed pretty early in the book so I don’t think I’m story flashing any of you with this information. I just think it is difficult to explain the novel without these two nuggets of bookformation. <br /><br />Therefore, for those of you that don’t want any spoilers, you may want to look away now…<br /><br />….for everyone else, let’s talk AMERICAN GODS….and English gods…and Irish gods…and Norse/Scandinavian gods…German and Russian gods…and Egyptian gods…and Greek gods (plus those “FUCKING Albanian” gods)…and Indian, Hindu and Japanese gods…and Hungarian gods…and Babylonian and Persian gods…and Native American gods…and Voodoo gods…and African gods….and even “forgotten” gods...plus all manner of dwarf, sprite, imp, giant, kobold, vampire, mythological beast, djinn, witch…and one very large and mysterious <strong>SHADOW</strong>!! Just listing the countries represented in this book makes me smile and break out in goose zits. <br /><br /><strong>THE TALE OF AMERICAN GODS</strong><br /><br />So our guide throughout the story is Shadow. Shadow’s a big, soft spoken, even tempered bad-ass, ex-con whose life is shattered by the tragic death of his wife in a car accident (there is more to it than that, but, you know, spoilers and such). While reeling from the aftermath of his loss, Shadow is approached by smooth-talking and mysterious grifter named Mr. Wednesday. <em>[HERE COMES MINOR SPOILER #1]</em>. <br /><br />Turns out, Wednesday is actually a manifestation of Odin, ruler of the Norse Gods, and king of all things Asgardian. <br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Book%20covers/?action=view&amp;current=odin-1.jpg">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380320723i/689956.jpg" /></a><br />Why you ask is the “AllFather,” the god of war, wisdom, poetry and magic scraping his way through life as an aging con man? Ah….that brings us to the heart and soul of the story. <br /><br />You see gods are sustained and kept strong, according to the novel, by people’s worship and their belief in them. Thus, when the ancient Norsemen came to America, they brought belief in their gods with them. When they made sacrifices to Odin, Thor and the rest of the Norse gods, it made them strong and powerful. Conversely, as the people forgot about the gods and stopped telling their tales and making offerings to them, their power waned, until now at the beginning of the 21st century in America, Odin’s godly “vigor” is all but lost. <br /><br />Meanwhile, the “gods” of the 21st century have grown strong and powerful. These new gods of Media, Technology, Internet, Electricity, Highways, Drugs, etc. are young, brash and dripping with vitality due to the worship and adoration they receive from you and me. Now, these godly young turkers and looking to destroy Odin and his ilk forever and claim supremacy over all of godness.<br /><br />A war is coming…sweet!!!<br /><br />Realizing the powerful of the 21st century gods, Odin is on recruiting mission to gather up the old gods and get them to sack up in order to avoid being slaughtered at the hands of the upstarts. He hires Shadow to be a glorified errand boy at $500 bucks a week and to accompany him as he travels America (and places in between) trying to rally his gang of gods to fight in the coming battle. <br /><br />From a broad brush perspective, that is really the frame for this novel. However, as with all great art, the beauty of this story is in the details, the aspects, the shadings, the nuances. Odin’s mission acts as a terrific catalyst for Gaiman to explore the history of America, his great love of mythology and the enormous power of belief. <blockquote>
  <em> ‘This is the only country in the world,’ said Wednesday, into the stillness, ‘that worries about what it is….The rest of them know what they are. No one ever needs to go searching for the heart of Norway. Or looks for the soul of Mozambique. They know what they are.’</em>
</blockquote><br />As with many of my favorite books, this is a “journey” story and not a “destination” story. Thus, if you’re someone who doesn’t like tangents, flashbacks, veer offs or segues in your plot and are constantly hoping to straight line towards the conclusion, than this book might frustrate you enough to cause hives. This is a beautiful, elegant, but long and meandering journey through the heart and soul of America full of rich and detailed landscapes, historical flashbacks, memorable characters and mythological anecdotes. <br /><br />Now, despite the novel taking its sweet, leisurely time sauntering towards the end, when it finally gets there, it is arguably my favorite 100 pages of any book EVER. In fact, the climax is so amazingly good that is it likely to cause one…so be prepared <br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Goodreads/?action=view&amp;current=UW_Men_Super_19842_L-XL_350-1.jpg">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1379765680i/239193.jpg" /></a> <br /><br />Of course, I am talking about the final dust up between the old and the new. This segment is filled with more gods and legendary creatures than I have ever seen assembled in one story (if you are a <u>South Park</u> fan, think <strong>Imagination Land</strong> and you will have an idea of the kind a concentrated star power I mean).<blockquote>
  <em>There's never been a true war that wasn't fought between two sets of people who were certain they were in the right. The really dangerous people believe they are doing whatever they are doing solely and only because it is without question the right thing to do. And that is what makes them dangerous.</em>
</blockquote><br /><strong>ADDITIONS TO THE PREFERRED TEXT</strong><br /><br />For those of you that have read the original and are wondering whether this expanded edition is worth your time, I say yes…with a caveat. I don’t see a need to go rush out and read this if you have just finished the original version of American Gods. The story is basically the same and the added text is not so extensive that they change the essence of the novel. However, it you are thinking of a re-read or have never read the story before, I would certainly recommend this edition as I think it provides some additional insight and clarifications that are interesting and worthwhile. Plus, this expanded version also includes a very neat “apocryphal” segment in the afterward showing Shadow meeting up with Jesus that I thought was interesting.<br /><br />Overall, I love this book. I have now read it three times (something I do not generally do with books) and I am fairly certain that a fourth time is in my future. If you love mythology, it is hard for me to imagine you not loving this book. One thing I would recommend is that you have handy either your own mythology guide or else a link to this website that lists all of the gods appearing in the novel. <a rel="nofollow" href="http://frowl.org/gods/gods.html#odin" target="_blank">http://frowl.org/gods/gods.html#odin</a><br /><br />I think it enhances the experience of the story significantly being able to match up the people encountered in the book with their mythological persona. 6.0 stars. HIGHEST POSSIBLE RECOMMENDATION!!<br /><br /><strong>P.S. <u>BONUE QUOTE</u></strong><br /><br />I couldn’t find a way to work this in above, but it is one of my favorite quotes from the book, so I wanted to share it: <blockquote>
  <em> God does not play dice with the universe; He plays an ineffable game of His own devising, which might be compared, from the perspective of the players, to being involved in an obscure and complex version of poker in a pitch-dark room, with blank cards, for infinite stakes, with a Dealer who won’t tell you the rules, and who smiles all the time.</em>
</blockquote><br /></span>

  <a href="/user/show/5300620-steve-sckenda" class="left imgcol" title="Steve Sckenda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1409027039p2/5300620.jpg" alt="Steve Sckenda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/441265125?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 15, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5300620-steve-sckenda" class="user" title="Steve Sckenda">Steve Sckenda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/983100.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780060558123" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Lovers of Fantasy</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Steve by: </span>
      30 Goodread Friends
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=mythology" class="actionLinkLite">mythology</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=hugo-award" class="actionLinkLite">hugo-award</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=nebula-award" class="actionLinkLite">nebula-award</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer441265125">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9912989093917409118"><strong> <em>What should I believe? Believe everything.</em> </strong><br /><br /><strong> <em>Belief without blood only takes us so far. The blood must flow.</em> </strong> <br /><br />This is no country for old gods. I drank the mead and surrendered to the power of dream logic of Neil Gaiman’s “American Gods,” which is a highly imaginative and well written novel that tells of how a diaspora of ancient gods organizes itself for battle against the new American gods of commerce and media. The paradigms are shifting, and gods have problems, too. Now I know.<br /><br />The United Stat</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9912989093917409118"><strong>
  <em>What should I believe? Believe everything.</em>
</strong><br /><br /><strong>
  <em>Belief without blood only takes us so far. The blood must flow.</em>
</strong> <br /><br />This is no country for old gods. I drank the mead and surrendered to the power of dream logic of Neil Gaiman’s “American Gods,” which is a highly imaginative and well written novel that tells of how a diaspora of ancient gods organizes itself for battle against the new American gods of commerce and media. The paradigms are shifting, and gods have problems, too. Now I know.<br /><br />The United States is a melting pot, and the immigrants to this New World imported towers of religious Babel with them. Gaiman tells the story of the arrival of the gods in the New World through interspersed vignettes about Vikings; indentured servants; slaves; and nomadic mammoth hunters. <br /><br />You might brush up on your Joseph Campbell because Norse, Egyptian, African, Native American, Eastern European myths are among some of the many ingredients in the American spiritual bouillabaisse. <br /><br />Though little knots of belief still exist, these are gods have passed out of collective memory. “Even their names are lost. Their totems are long since broken and cast down. Their last priests died without passing on their secrets.” <br /><br />Because nobody sacrifices to these ancient gods, the gods resort to shabby grift. They are analog gods in a digital world; immaterial beings in a material world. But the gods will no longer be ignored. <br /><br />We encounter these gods through the main character, Shadow, who is awaiting release from prison at the opening of the novel. Shadow’s eyes are being opened by suffering. Shadow “was no longer scared of what tomorrow might bring because yesterday had brought it.” He was a bullied boy whose mother died while he was a kid, and he became a widower in his twenties after his wife died a sudden violent death. <br /><br />The novel opens with Shadow in prison, where he minds his own business ("Do your own time; not the time of others"), and he practices coin tricks. He reads Herodotus, “the Father of History,” who wrote “historical truth” about gods interacting with men. What do reading, religion, and coin tricks have in common? What skills do they teach Shadow?<br /><br />Now dreams come to him: buffalo men; rock-skull mountains; thunderbirds; and eaglestones. His dead wife challenges him: “you’re not alive, but you’re not dead either.” What does it all mean? Buffalo Man warns him, “If you are to survive you must believe. Believe What? Every Thing." <br /><br />Shadow asks,“which path should I take. Which one is safe?” The answer: “Neither path is safe. Which way would you walk the way of hard truths or the way of fine lies?” Shadow responds “Truths. I’ve come too far for more lies.” I respond with Shadow: "I’ll pay the price of truth. I’ll pay it." By the way, What’s the price? <br /><br />I raced through the pages and absorbed the tone of menace, leavened with humor. (I found Sam's speech about what she believes to be hilarious and have selected it as a quotation in its entirety below.)<br /><br />I have not read a lot of fantasy outside LOTR, and I came to this book solely through goodreads recommendations. More than 30 of my friends read this book, and it resonated strongly with the vast majority of them. I wholeheartedly concur with their assessment. It comforts me to belong to a community of perceptive readers who are open to dream magic and who see valuable metaphors for our lives— battered and perplexed by mystery though they are.<br /><br /><strong>Tire not of mystery, says Gaiman—it adds a “zest to the world like salt in a stew.” </strong> Gods are great. But the heart is greater. “For it is from our hearts they come, and to our hearts they shall return.” I read somewhere else that the Kingdom of God is within us, so I keep looking and reading to make sense of this mystery of life. <br /><br />Thank you, my friends, for helping me on this journey by directing my attention to quality modern literature that provides signposts, metaphors, and archetypes. I need you as I need that mystery and my books.<br /><br />November 4, 2012</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10374145178843529398">Your review stirred up the recesses of my memory, reminding me of a fantasy trilogy I read ages ago when I started reading in English. It's the <em>Abhors</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10374145178843529398">Your review stirred up the recesses of my memory, reminding me of a fantasy trilogy I read ages ago when I started reading in English. It's the <em>Abhorsen</em> trilogy by <a title="Garth Nix" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8347.Garth_Nix">Garth Nix</a>, whose main character has to deal with the loss of family and come to terms with her identity while facing burdensome responsibilities. The undercurrent themes of sacrifice, destiny and death blend together to give a hopeful message to what you call in your review <em>"mystery of life."</em>. You have a way to touch all the keys of these never-ending piano of literature, while listening to other singing voices, and never fail to provide the most harmonic and melodious of nocturnes.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_420865066">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/420865066" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1444651-david-katzman" class="left imgcol" title="David Katzman"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1399048696p2/1444651.jpg" alt="David Katzman" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/420865066?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 26, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1444651-david-katzman" class="user" title="David Katzman">David Katzman</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4407.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780747263746" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer420865066">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5787653070442581369">I find myself shocked at the awards this book has won and the praise heaped upon it. How on Gods’ Earth could a book about Gods walking on the Earth among mortals be so pedestrian? Somehow Gaiman managed to turn a potentially cool premise into something boring. For those who love this book—and I know it is many—please forgive the sarcasm to follow as I blaspheme against the beloved Gaiman. But Gods help me, the more I read, the more I hated <em>American Gods</em>.<br /><br />First off, while the premise sounds inter</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5787653070442581369">I find myself shocked at the awards this book has won and the praise heaped upon it. How on Gods’ Earth could a book about Gods walking on the Earth among mortals be so pedestrian? Somehow Gaiman managed to turn a potentially cool premise into something boring. For those who love this book—and I know it is many—please forgive the sarcasm to follow as I blaspheme against the beloved Gaiman. But Gods help me, the more I read, the more I hated <em>American Gods</em>.<br /><br />First off, while the premise sounds interesting the more I thought about it, the less I liked it. The basic idea: the more worshippers a God has, the more powerful they are. The plot: there is a building power struggle between the old Gods (Norse, Native American, pagan, etc.) and the new Gods (Technology, Television, Money, etc.). Okay, I’ve heard the ratio-of-worshippers-to-power idea before so that’s not so original. But it’s not a deal breaker. It has potential. Here’s the unique twist in <em>American Gods</em> that caused my political antenna to start twitching—every God (like say Odin) has an “avatar” of him or herself in each country. Or is it each continent? Gaiman’s not quite clear about that. Would there be an Odin in Belgium <em>and</em> Luxembourg? Or does all of Europe get one Odin who is different from the American Odin? I find it politically disagreeable to suggest that every country (or even continent) has different God-avatars. To make this the premise turns intangible political entities (nations) into strictly bordered spiritual containers. It’s parochial thinking. I disagree with this premise radically because I reject that people of a given “nation” are somehow bonded spiritually. Countries are artificial. Like Afghanistan. Like how we stole the native people’s land to form America. I ascribe to the perspective that while people should always be fighting for political freedom and better political systems locally and nationally, we are truly citizens of the world together. The premise of <em>American Gods</em> manages to privilege the people in one country as somehow being united in their spiritual energy, feeding the Gods only within that country. As a metaphor (Gaiman repeatedly feels the need to state that this premise is a metaphor) it fails. There should be no metaphorical boundary between my spirit and my sister’s and brother’s spirits in Nicaragua, even if we have different local needs. Further, I could go on about how old Gods (religious deities) are in cahoots with modern Gods like wealth and technology. Just look at the fact that all the evangelists support the party of the 1%.<br /><br />Political oversensitivity on my part aside, the rant continues.<br /><br />The main character, Shadow, was about the dullest hero I’ve ever read. For Gods’ sake how many times do other characters have to refer to how “big” he is? Is he a big man? He sure is big. Wow, you’re big. Apparently he’s big. Is he big? Oh boy is he a big man. Yep, he’s big. He was big and boring and one-dimensional. So pure of heart that it grated on me. I found the majority of his dialogue to be trite and conventional. He struck me throughout as a pawn of the author (and yes he was a pawn of the Gods, too) more than a real being. His words were missing that spark of believability to bring the character to life. I didn’t even believe his repeated sleight-of-hand behavior. It felt like a character trait on a chart that Gaiman could pull out every couple of chapters. And when it came to the other God characters? I just wasn’t feelin’ it. They seemed phony as all get-out. I did not find his representation of them credible. I think my reaction to their characterizations were primarily due to a reaction to mediocre dialogue. The dialogue wasn’t awful, but I found it to be consistently off—slightly awkward, slightly unnatural, subtly stilted.<br /><br />Most of the story was told in very close third person from Shadow’s point-of-view. But every once in a while, Gaiman would throw in a chapter from another character’s point-of-view. These chapters read in some ways like short stories inserted into the novel to expurgate some backstory, elucidate the God/worshipper premise in more detail, or delve into a side character. I find such techniques utterly amateurish. One or two “interludes” in a book might be acceptable but to have an entire story driving in a close third person POV and then jump into another character because you can’t “explain something” from the primary POV is cheap. It’s an easy out. I react badly when authors feel the need to “explain things” to begin with. And to interrupt the flow of the structure you’ve created to do so pisses me off. It made me feel as though Gaiman were talking down to me as the reader, like I was a little kid who didn’t get it. Or like his storytelling just wasn’t good enough to tell the story without jumping out of it to explain it. Understanding should come organically. Or else the POV jumping should happen more frequently, such as, every chapter. It’s all about rhythm of storytelling.<br /><br />Swathes of <em>American Gods</em> were just plain boring. About 2/3 of the way through I started skipping whole paragraphs, then pages to get to plot events. All the stuff between the plot events was trying my patience. Shadow spends a great deal of time stuck in a small town in northern Wisconsin, meeting all these good-hearted locals and exploring bits of small-town life. I felt like I was stuck in a small town in northern Wisconsin during the winter the whole time. I’m like—this is not freaking <em>Housekeeping</em> and Gaiman sure ain’t Marilynne Robinson. He does not have the writing chops to pull off an intimate look at real small-town life.<br /><br />Modest spoiler: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[The entire chapter where the old Gods meet the new Gods in truce made no logical sense. Even if the place they met was neutral due to its magical qualities, the new Gods simply had to track the trucks when the old Gods drove off and bomb the hell out of them. It was just this weird excuse to have some conversations between the old and new, between Shadow and the new Gods. And to get that body back. Contrived. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Oh yeah, and if you tell me over and over again that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[a war is coming, <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> a big, big, big <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[fucking war <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> is coming, then you better give me a big fucking <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[war <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Guess what? What do you think?<br /><br />Big spoiler here: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[Let me see if I can summarize the speech at the climax, which prevented the war from happening. Shadow finds out that his father set up the war all for his own gain. So he goes in front of all the Gods … what is he going to say? How is he going to stop this horrendous war from occurring? What could he possibly say?!?! Well, he proceeds to explain to them that his father set up the war all for his own gain. Wow. I can’t believe he pulled that off. Except by just explaining it. Which he did. Great. Thrilling. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />By the end, I was ready to shoot <em>American Gods</em> but I had to wade through an epilogue <em>and</em> a postscript. It was like a pimple on top of a wart. But I guess I’m not surprised that he wanted to tie up loose ends after the climax because he couldn’t figure out how to do it during the story itself. Bah. <br /><br />I viscerally disliked this book. I think it’s because as a whole it felt emotionally manipulative. Such a charge could have been avoided with living, breathing characters. But despite the transparent planning and plotting, none of it rang true. Even Fantasy, characters need to feel real. These didn’t.<!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5787653070442581369">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/452748081?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 11, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin" class="user" title="Bill  Kerwin">Bill  Kerwin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4407.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780747263746" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=weird-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">weird-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer452748081">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17497701535241475414"><br />In this unique love letter to the United States, Gaiman manages to celebrate its underground spiritual traditions, glory in the magnificence of its landmarks, landscapes, and bizarre tourist traps, and--most important--both mourn and venerate its pagan (often immigrant) gods in decline, battered and diminished though they may be by the shallowness and speed of a technological world. The gods are indeed the best part of this very good book: they are degenerate and threadbare, and yet still gods,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17497701535241475414"><br />In this unique love letter to the United States, Gaiman manages to celebrate its underground spiritual traditions, glory in the magnificence of its landmarks, landscapes, and bizarre tourist traps, and--most important--both mourn and venerate its pagan (often immigrant) gods in decline, battered and diminished though they may be by the shallowness and speed of a technological world. The gods are indeed the best part of this very good book: they are degenerate and threadbare, and yet still gods, capable of inspiring allegiance and terror. <br /><br />Gaiman loves not only fantasy, but also mystery and horror, and here he has constructed a book which fulfills the genre requirements of all. The plot is complicated and crammed with marvels: the beginning promises pleasures and horrors, the middle disturbs the balance, and the ending surprises and yet satisfies. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17497701535241475414">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_7174736">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7174736" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/16958-dan" class="left imgcol" title="Dan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1182804964p2/16958.jpg" alt="Dan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/7174736?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 23, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/16958-dan" class="user" title="Dan">Dan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4407.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780747263746" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">americans, gods, and everyone inbetween</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer7174736">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14331734006067581219">This book (in a very round about way) taught me what good literature is. My mother was telling me about this book, and commented that it is good literature. Now, I was surprised to hear this because Neil Gaiman is usually a nonstop sex and violence party of disaffected goth teenager fantasy. Furthermore, I didn't really believe in good literature. I had had so much obvious bull-plop literary analysis crammed down my throat in high school (A high school teacher once said to our class "In the Grea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14331734006067581219">This book (in a very round about way) taught me what good literature is. My mother was telling me about this book, and commented that it is good literature. Now, I was surprised to hear this because Neil Gaiman is usually a nonstop sex and violence party of disaffected goth teenager fantasy. Furthermore, I didn't really believe in good literature. I had had so much obvious bull-plop literary analysis crammed down my throat in high school (A high school teacher once said to our class "In the Great Gatsby, green means go, FOR SEX!") I'm a logical type of dude, and such literary analysis seemed like wildly speculative and unfounded pondering. So I asked my mom, what makes good literature and she said two things make good literature:<br /><br />(1) Language must be used in an artistic way.<br />(2) There must be serious themes or metaphors in the work.<br /><br />And this, for the first time ever, made literature make sense to me.<br /><br />By this metric, American Gods is good literature. This book is very well written. The language is complex but it flows well and reads easily. The description is deep and enveloping, but does not make the reading labored.<br /><br />This book is about how the old gods, brought to America in the myth and beliefs of immigrants for thousands of years, are alive on the fringes of our society. Our culture has no time for them, and they wither and grow old in the lack of worship. These gods, such as Anansi, Easter, and The Queen of Sheba, can practice magic; but they are relegated to the position of grifters, criminals, con men, and prostitutes. Meanwhile, the new gods, gods of technology, internet, wall street, and media are becoming strong and want to kill off the old gods. This book is the story of their war.<br /><br />Of course, the book is rife with themes and metaphors about religion and America in general. And everyone has a take on what it all means. You can read many people here on Good Reads' take on it. You can read mine below, I don't want to affect your opinion about the book if you don't want me to. <br /><br />I read this book because my mom recommended it, because I like Neil Gaiman, and because I wanted to see what it was all about. <br /><br />WARNING THEMATIC DISCOVERY SPOILER (could ruin the fun, as unwrapping the metaphor of this book is as fun if not more so than discovering the plot.)<br /><br />I think that this book is about the cultural shift in America that has been occurring over the last quarter century. The specific cultural shift of which I speak is the shift to a country wide homogeneous culture. Mom and Pop stores have been giving way to Walmart. Local restaurants give way to McDonald's, Subway, Olive Garden, or the Cheese Cake Factory. Local television stations are no longer watched in favor of Digital Cable or Satellite Television. Local Radio stations are bought up by clear channel and local or idiosyncratic programming is scrubbed in favor of nation wide play lists. Ethnic or local cultures are assimilated into the new American culture.<br /><br />The old gods are metaphors of the ethnic immigrant and local cultures in America. The stories about how each god came to America are not about the gods themselves, they are about the immigrants who believe in them, and how they came to the new world. <br /><br />The new gods represent the culturally homogenizing forces in America. They are the internet, the media, credit, and technology.<br /><br />Repeatedly <a title="Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Gaiman">Gaiman</a> refers to "the battle for the soul of America" between the new and old gods. This battle represents the assimilation of the American people into the new homogeneous culture and how this causes us to for go our ethnic backgrounds.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14331734006067581219">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
