<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1375635877">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1375635877" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/27788046-bookworm-sean" class="left imgcol" title="Bookworm Sean"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1408905793p2/27788046.jpg" alt="Bookworm Sean" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1375635877?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 11, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/27788046-bookworm-sean" class="user" title="Bookworm Sean">Bookworm Sean</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4407.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780747263746" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">my enemies </span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/27788046-bookworm-sean?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/27788046-bookworm-sean?shelf=i-hate-you" class="actionLinkLite">i-hate-you</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1375635877">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10414451244112477381">Do you ever read a book and become completely lost in the words and, ultimately, wonder what is actually happening? Well, I do. So, I go back and read the bits I may not have picked up or accidently skimmed over. This allows me to actually understand the book. I tried doing that with this, and I quickly realised that I <u>still</u> had no idea what was going on. The plot of this felt completely random, drawn out to the point of ridiculousness and the events, themselves, felt incoherent. I have no idea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10414451244112477381">Do you ever read a book and become completely lost in the words and, ultimately, wonder what is actually happening? Well, I do. So, I go back and read the bits I may not have picked up or accidently skimmed over. This allows me to actually understand the book. I tried doing that with this, and I quickly realised that I <u>still</u> had no idea what was going on. The plot of this felt completely random, drawn out to the point of ridiculousness and the events, themselves, felt incoherent. I have no idea why most of the events actually happened in here, and at this point, I can honestly say that just I don’t care anymore. <br /><br /><strong> Did I miss something? </strong><br /><br />The book begins with the protagonist, Shadow, finishing his prison sentence. On the day of release his wife is killed in a car accident. What initially appears as mere bad fortune slowly evolves into what can only be considered as something much odder. His wife’s ghost visits him, and assists him in the random events he then encounters. I say random because that’s exactly what this book is. The events that occurred had no discernible point. I kept expecting to see some reasoning behind it all, but just couldn’t. <br /><br />Perhaps I missed something. But the plot of this felt barely connected. There was an overall lack of cohesion and plot driver. I had very little reason to read this, and as I got further and further into it, I had even less. The book seemed to be going in a weird direction of its own that felt completely ungraspable. I just don’t understand the point of most of it; the characters all felt like they belonged in a psyche ward. I understand the overall meaning of the book, but the way in which the author presented it was awful. The actual events and scenes that took place were bizarre to the point of them having no purpose. For me, this book needed much more than just an overall juxtaposition of god types; it needed to be enjoyable on the surface level as well; it needed a proper plot. <br /><br /><strong> This book almost killed me </strong> <br /><br /><img width="500" height="281" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1441890610ra/16165627.gif" /><br /><br />If a book bores me this much, and confuses me this much, when reading, the overall message of the book cannot save it in my estimation. The reading process was dull and plain arduous, I wanted to cry at points because it was that bad. Indeed, I had to force myself to complete reading this incredibly packed out, and rambling piece of randomness. If someone asked me to give a concise summary of the book, and tell them what happened, I’d be unable to complete the task. Perhaps it’s just me, but this book is so strange. I have nothing positive to say about it, in any respect, and absolutely hated reading it. I just can’t appreciate what Gainman was trying to achieve because he did it such a roundabout way. <br /><br />I simply detest this book. It was an absolute trudge to finish it. This just seemed far too long. The message that the author was trying to capture could have been done in half the word count. Perhaps, it doesn’t help that my copy was the original version, which means its twelve thousand words longer than the normal one. For me, this meant that there were entire chapters that were completely pointless. Nothing happened in them, and nothing was achieved through them. At points, this novel felt like a connected series of events that could barely be considered a plot. It will, indeed, be many months before I pick up another book by this author, maybe even years, maybe not ever again. I’ll never forgive the author for this tripe. <br /><br /><strong> A very disheartened one star </strong></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10414451244112477381">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_274051935">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/274051935" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday" class="left imgcol" title="mark monday"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1446402303p2/131922.jpg" alt="mark monday" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/274051935?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 07, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday" class="user" title="mark monday">mark monday</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4407.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780747263746" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=mythopoeikon" class="actionLinkLite">mythopoeikon</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=secret-histories" class="actionLinkLite">secret-histories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer274051935">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9210333910289114286">a protagonist, Shadow. calm, collected, quiet, passive, cagey, a tough guy and a sensitive guy. his life has been about reacting and not impacting. he moves through his story as if through a dream; tragedies and betrayals and mysteries and confidence games, the beginnings and endings of hope and love and life - all viewed as if through water, as if these terrible wonders were happening to someone else. he could be nothing more than a pawn in life - let alone a pawn of the gods - but yet his pass</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9210333910289114286">a protagonist, Shadow. calm, collected, quiet, passive, cagey, a tough guy and a sensitive guy. his life has been about reacting and not impacting. he moves through his story as if through a dream; tragedies and betrayals and mysteries and confidence games, the beginnings and endings of hope and love and life - all viewed as if through water, as if these terrible wonders were happening to someone else. he could be nothing more than a pawn in life - let alone a pawn of the gods - but yet his passivity is combined with an inner strength, a gentle kindness, an innate decency. his decency is more than a trait - it defines him. and when he does decide to act, his actions become another one of this novel's terrible wonders. he is complex and completely appealing. one of my my favorite heroes in modern fiction.<br /><br />a wife, Laura. dead before the novel begins. a living ghost, a revenant; aided by a magical coin. a sad and appalling and dreamlike journey. a terrific payoff.<br /><br />a Road Trip, full of kitchen-sink reality, full of terrible wonder.<br /><br />an old guard, the Old Gods. Odin, Anansi, Czernobog, Anubis and Thoth and Bast and Horus, Eostre, Kali, the Norns. the Queen of Sheba - strangely, now an Old God. their transformation into prosaic american emigres - confidence men and tenement dwellers and whores and jailhouse cellmates - is both a fun game of mystery-solving (guess the god!) and a rather sad parallel to how the world of the not-aged pushes the aged into their little corners, out of sight and out of mind, into a world of irrelevance. and yet the old gods are not portrayed with cloying sweetness - there is no filter of nostalgia or hints of some long-lost, longed-for Olden Way to make us love them. they are fascinating, powerful, often monstrous, entirely without easy sentiment.<br /><br />a new guard, the New Gods. Technology, the Media, the Vehicle, the Men in Black. it's easy to put them all automatically in a nice little box labeled The Villains. but that's too simple; too instantly gratifying. the novel's portrait of these potentially repellent figures is one painted in broad strokes, lacking subtlety - but it is also a fair one... they sound and act like villains, and yet they are not. how can they be? we worship them on a daily basis. they are only evil if we consider our modern age an evil one.<br /><br />a creepy subsidiary villain: a Kobold. he lives in a portrait of classic americana that miraculously manages to be free of condescension.<br /><br />a Confidence Game, long-game style, played by two gods, with our hero as their catspaw. cleverly done by the gods. beautifully overturned by our hero. brilliantly pulled off by the author.<br /><br />an author, Neil Gaiman. i'll admit that i have well-stocked reserves of love for the man, ready and willing to be accessed. his Sandman series is my favorite comic book, full of crazy imagination and complex mysteries and creepy villains and complicated heroes and terrible, wonderful journeys. so i was prepared to love this book as i love its author, to draw upon that deep well of affection. there were connecting factors that helped to ease my transition from dealing with my old god of awesome comics to this new literary god of popular fantasy fiction; namely his interest in unearthing and revitalizing the world's myths and legends - an ongoing Gaiman leitmotif. i was less prepared to discover that Gaiman is an actual writer! his prose is fluid, nuanced, supple, at ease with both constant ambiguity and you-are-there, detailed realism. both grounded and hallucinatory. the narrative is anchored by everyday reality as well as a strange lack of affect - a blankness of tone that is a good fit for a phantasmagorical road trip. and then it is capable of sudden, surreal flights of visionary madness, a grey-toned package that becomes a conjurer's box of magic. and then that tricksy legerdemain can just as quickly transform back into a soulful and emotionally honest portrait of one man's journey through various adventures, through his own life, to the end of it and back again. who could ask for more?<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9210333910289114286">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1671667">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1671667" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/94602-kelly" class="left imgcol" title="Kelly"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1437154881p2/94602.jpg" alt="Kelly" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1381598023">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1381598023" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/53165-warwick" class="left imgcol" title="Warwick"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1356073672p2/53165.jpg" alt="Warwick" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1381598023?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 08, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/53165-warwick" class="user" title="Warwick">Warwick</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/13101468-american-gods" title="Paperback 9780062080233" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=united-states" class="actionLinkLite">united-states</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1381598023">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5753093513322722151">I find it really weird how many American media products have the word "American" in the title. Obviously, this; a few weeks back I also read <a title="American Rust by Philipp Meyer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4251902.American_Rust">American Rust</a>. You've got your <em>American Beauty</em>, <em>American Ninja</em>, <em>An American Werewolf in London</em>. <a title="American Psycho by Bret Easton Ellis" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28676.American_Psycho">American Psycho</a>. <a title="American Sniper by Chris Kyle" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11887020.American_Sniper">American Sniper</a>. <em>American Pie</em>, <em>American Dad</em>, <em>American Graffiti</em>. What is going on here, what are they trying to prove?? I really don't understand it. I mean you'd never get "British Beauty", "French Psycho", would you? That just seems completely l</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5753093513322722151">I find it really weird how many American media products have the word "American" in the title. Obviously, this; a few weeks back I also read <a title="American Rust by Philipp Meyer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4251902.American_Rust">American Rust</a>. You've got your <em>American Beauty</em>, <em>American Ninja</em>, <em>An American Werewolf in London</em>. <a title="American Psycho by Bret Easton Ellis" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28676.American_Psycho">American Psycho</a>. <a title="American Sniper by Chris Kyle" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11887020.American_Sniper">American Sniper</a>. <em>American Pie</em>, <em>American Dad</em>, <em>American Graffiti</em>. What is going on here, what are they trying to prove?? I really don't understand it. I mean you'd never get "British Beauty", "French Psycho", would you? That just seems completely laughable.<br /><br />Anyway, I really didn't get this book. It made no sense to me at all. I mean it's a fun conceit, that gods are living among men in modern day America, desperate to regain the faith they once commanded, but I just felt like it wasn't thought through properly. It presents itself as being predicated on the idea that ‘America is a bad land for gods’ – this is something that characters keep saying to one another, moodily, that America is a really bad land for gods – and this is apparently why all the gods are now living hand-to-mouth existences as drifters or menial labourers.<br /><br />Only – huh? Are we talking about the same America here? The one where 51 percent of the population think that humans were created by a divine being, and a further 40 percent think they were created by evolution which was set in motion by a divine being (leaving, as Tim Minchin said, a very small percentage of Americans who are right)? Is that the America that is supposed to be a bad land for gods? Do me a favour, it must be one of the most religious countries in the western world. I've driven through my share of rural Tennessee, where much of <em>American Gods</em> takes place, and one of the most striking things about these communities is the fact that there seems to be one church for every six or seven houses. God is invoked on the currency, on the news, by the head of state, and in schoolrooms every morning by little kids.<br /><br />This is what is so frustrating about the book, because it seems like a brilliant chance to examine religion in the US in a cool and interesting way – but it doesn't. It either doesn't dare or it doesn't bother. I mean – if you're going to run with this idea that gods are walking around, with the more powerful deities being those who have the most believers, then where the fuck is Yahweh? I'm supposed to believe that Anubis is twatting around driving a hearse in fucking Cairo, IL. – despite the fact that no one in the history of America has ever worshipped Anubis – and yet Jesus doesn't make a single appearance? Somehow it's OK to play around with foreign gods that seem quaint or folkloric, but monotheism's off the table. It just didn't make any sense to me.¹<br /><br />Instead, what we have to propel the narrative along is just a kind of comic-book war that we're supposed to care about. So although there were quite a few scenes that had me flipping the pages with engagement, there was always this nagging feeling that none of it really meant anything and that I didn't really care very much what happened to anyone. It doesn't help that the protagonist (with the dreadful name of ‘Shadow Moon’) is, for a central character, annoyingly passive and lacking in personality (although the goddesses he encounters still have a remarkable habit of wanting to have sex with him).<br /><br />As for the writing style, well, it's fine, but it has absolutely no flair. There's quite an interesting bit in the Acknowledgements where Gaiman thanks many of his beta-readers and editors for spotting ‘stray and unintentional anglicisms’, presumably so he could remove them; this I think is something that contributes to the featureless blandness of his style. I'm not saying he is unentitled to this voice or anything like that – his wife is American, he lives in America, this is totally an authentic voice for him. It's just not one that has any character. It works in a kind of tab-A-into-slot-B way.<br /><br />This is certainly not a bad book and it's quite readable – I think I'm just disappointed because I had unfairly high hopes, and I liked the concept, and I have a lot of friends who really enjoyed it. For me it was just a bit baffling and cartoony. In the same way that <em>His Dark Materials</em> is like a children's story for grown-ups, <em>American Gods</em> felt like an adult story for children. This is my third Neil Gaiman book (after <em>Sandman</em> and <em>Smoke and Mirrors</em>) and they have all been underwhelming; I think I'll just leave him alone now, since I'm sure they deserve higher ratings than I'm prepared to give them, but that's what you get when I try and squeeze in a review at 01:23 am in a foreign city when I still have another two hours' work to do before I can go to bed.<br /><br /><br />¹This "Tenth Anniversary Edition" includes in its appendices a brief section in which Jesus does, in fact make a brief appearance. This was cut from the original published version, and you can see why; it is very short and it raises more questions than it answers. The problem is, these are the questions the book should have been about. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5753093513322722151">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_15968899">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15968899" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/175635-trevor" class="left imgcol" title="Trevor"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1388976384p2/175635.jpg" alt="Trevor" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/15968899?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 03, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/175635-trevor" class="user" title="Trevor">Trevor</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4407.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780747263746" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/175635-trevor?shelf=literature" class="actionLinkLite">literature</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/175635-trevor?shelf=religion" class="actionLinkLite">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer15968899">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14666749255747326640">I did like this, I liked this very much.<br /><br />This was on my to read list and given I’ve never heard of the guy before it must have been recommended to me by someone. No idea who, though. It is a little surprising that when I looked no one I knew had reviewed this book. What had inspired me to read it is lost now.<br /><br />A friend of mine wrote to me last week about her son’s interest in magic tricks – now, that must be the first time in years that I’ve thought about magic at all. So, when this one started and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14666749255747326640">I did like this, I liked this very much.<br /><br />This was on my to read list and given I’ve never heard of the guy before it must have been recommended to me by someone. No idea who, though. It is a little surprising that when I looked no one I knew had reviewed this book. What had inspired me to read it is lost now.<br /><br />A friend of mine wrote to me last week about her son’s interest in magic tricks – now, that must be the first time in years that I’ve thought about magic at all. So, when this one started and the main character turns out to like doing coin tricks I had that strange feeling one sometimes gets when a couple of unexpected lines cross just as your passing and catch your heel so as to make you stumble and skip for a step or two.<br /><br />There is a Gary Larson cartoon stacked full of all of the gods that no one believes in anymore – I can barely remember it – some sort of retirement home for forgotten gods. If you took that cartoon, stirred in some Tom Waits, added a dash or two of Joseph Campbell, sprinkled a handful of jet black humour, creamed in a murder mystery and plopped a few cubes of Houdini’s more impressive escape acts you might have some idea what this book is like.<br /><br />More than once I laughed with pure delight, though more often I just smiled at how nicely this was written and how well the story was handled. Okay, it nearly got away from him towards the end and there was probably just a bit too much talking to camera at one point which is always the least impressive way to explain a major plotting point – even after all this had been handled so much competently (ie trusting the reader more) earlier in the book – I still loved this book.<br /><br />The idea of the Gods being virtually unemployed and eking out a living on road kill or through prostitution was very amusing. Some of the asides were as interesting as the story itself. Who would have thought that Genies would be such queer things?<br /><br />I’m not really one to read fantasy novels, but this one was quite something. Clever, fast paced and with themes of life, death, love, revenge, obligation, gratitude, desire, loss and the right time for things to happen – how could I not like this book?<br /><br />I don't believe in God or Gods or so many things - but in fiction, when a mysterious character says, "Believe"... When that happens, I believe. I don't need Gods, but I do need myths and stories and the psychological truths they reveal. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14666749255747326640">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1009185382">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1009185382" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5451856-tash" class="left imgcol" title="Tash"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1449738749p2/5451856.jpg" alt="Tash" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1009185382?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 07, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5451856-tash" class="user" title="Tash">Tash</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4407.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780747263746" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5451856-tash?shelf=urban-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5451856-tash?shelf=take-my-money-now" class="actionLinkLite">take-my-money-now</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5451856-tash?shelf=2016-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2016-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1009185382">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4209039277083444370">For a confused minute (<em>or a week really if I can be truly honest with myself</em>), I tried typing <strong>power overwhelming</strong> using my Kindle Paperwhite. Now kids, power overwhelming is a Star Craft cheat that made ones' troops god-like or invincible. I wanted god-like powers so I'd have the power to read through and like American Gods, but alas, I am only human and cannot smite Neil Gaiman for my disappointment.<br /><br />I have to hand it to Neil Gaiman for coming up with this fantastic concept, a Battle Royale betwe</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4209039277083444370">For a confused minute (<em>or a week really if I can be truly honest with myself</em>), I tried typing <strong>power overwhelming</strong> using my Kindle Paperwhite. Now kids, power overwhelming is a Star Craft cheat that made ones' troops god-like or invincible. I wanted god-like powers so I'd have the power to read through and like American Gods, but alas, I am only human and cannot smite Neil Gaiman for my disappointment.<br /><br />I have to hand it to Neil Gaiman for coming up with this fantastic concept, a Battle Royale between the old and the new gods. What's not to like? Plenty as it turns out. I know I am not alone in my disappointment with American Gods.I buddy read this with my friends <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1383468340?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1">Vane</a> and Evelyn (who hasn't finished yet), but Vane and I have pretty much similar gripes. On the bigger scale though, we're probably the odd ducks as this is such a popular and critically acclaimed novel.<br /><br />One of the biggest issues I have with American Gods is its main protagonist, <strong>Shadow</strong>. He's a lug with a penchant for coin tricks and is dull. I'm not sure if he's really meant to be that way to contrast with the bigger-than-life deities or he's just an underdeveloped character, either way it worked against the narrative of the story. The old deities were just a little better written than Shadow, while the new gods were laughably undeveloped. I didn't care for any of them at all.<br />Early on in the book the end-goal is discussed at some length, so I was filled with anticipation as to whom will join their fight, but that anticipation withered as the story dragged on to anywhere but the fight. I had to read 10% each day as per our buddy read contract (emoji filled gchat really, but who cares), but most days I was just like<br /><br /><img width="805" height="402" class="escapedImg" alt="lunch chipotle hungry are we done" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1452196206ra/17660237.gif" /><br /><br />At around 80%, I was all<br /><br /><img width="500" height="282" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1452196206ra/17660238.gif" /><br /><br />It's a shame really as the plot was really good, but the execution was just TOO tedious.<br /><br />I'm sure the word weird has been thrown a lot when talking about American Gods. Oh, it is plenty weird, but the world itself is not weird as much as muddled and poorly incorporated. I guess when gods walk among men, everything else is fair game, it doesn't matter if mortals can grasp it or not. It certainly takes Deus Ex Machina to a different level.<br /><br />This is my first Gaiman and I'm not impressed yet.Normally, I'm a fan of the weird, the strange and fantastical, this is not one of those times.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4209039277083444370">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_5287751">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5287751" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/282936-wade" class="left imgcol" title="Wade"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1452007011p2/282936.jpg" alt="Wade" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/5287751?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 29, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/282936-wade" class="user" title="Wade">Wade</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4407.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780747263746" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer5287751">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4457132059887340429">i'm a graduate student in theology, so how can i not love this book?<br />this book is one of the most creative descriptions of my own understanding of theology. gods do not exist on some eternal plane, but they rise and fall with the cultures and peoples who support and worship them. these gods have avatars in many different places--they are not a single entity but many that are called by the same name. mythologies can be more true than reality. and it's a good warning about how careful we should be</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4457132059887340429">i'm a graduate student in theology, so how can i not love this book?<br />this book is one of the most creative descriptions of my own understanding of theology. gods do not exist on some eternal plane, but they rise and fall with the cultures and peoples who support and worship them. these gods have avatars in many different places--they are not a single entity but many that are called by the same name. mythologies can be more true than reality. and it's a good warning about how careful we should be when we try to make our gods into simple human form. this is a theme that fascinates me (coming as i do from the Christian tradition). you'll see a number of books about Christian fascism on my bookshelf, and there's a theme. i just finished The Kite Runner, so i'm also thinking about the Taliban's attempts to bring their understanding of god into concrete human reality. it gets messy, and i'm not convinced it's a good thing. yet in this book, Gaiman also provides us with gods in human form who are good, fun, and mostly ambiguous.<br />i guess for me, American Gods is a good cautionary tale, for me, about how gods lurk in our subconscious, and about our need to dethrone divine violence in favor of other models of sacredness. we need to be careful what and how and who we worship in the ways we choose to live.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4457132059887340429">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_175644806">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175644806" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5611008-kay" class="left imgcol" title="Kay"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1314169555p2/5611008.jpg" alt="Kay" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/175644806?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 29, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5611008-kay" class="user" title="Kay">Kay</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/983100.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780060558123" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5611008-kay?shelf=urban-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5611008-kay?shelf=all-time-favorites" class="actionLinkLite">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5611008-kay?shelf=quirky" class="actionLinkLite">quirky</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5611008-kay?shelf=journey-not-destination" class="actionLinkLite">journey-not-destination</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5611008-kay?shelf=life-after-death" class="actionLinkLite">life-after-death</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5611008-kay?shelf=6-plus-stars" class="actionLinkLite">6-plus-stars</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5611008-kay?shelf=gods-and-beasts" class="actionLinkLite">gods-and-beasts</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5611008-kay?shelf=mythological" class="actionLinkLite">mythological</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer175644806">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16113937865334858239">This book turned me over completely to Neil Gaiman and made me drooly and ga-ga for his writing like a Twilight fangirl on too much fairy dust. I've read a few of Gaiman's works before (<a title="Preludes and Nocturnes (The Sandman, #1) by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6657541.Preludes_and_Nocturnes__The_Sandman___1_">The Sandman Vol. 1: Preludes and Nocturnes</a>, <a title="Neverwhere by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14497.Neverwhere">Neverwhere</a>, <a title="Anansi Boys by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2744.Anansi_Boys">Anansi Boys</a>, and <a title="Stardust by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16793.Stardust">Stardust</a>), but none have blown me so completely out of the boiling seas like American Gods. <br /><br />American Gods is one of the quirkiest books on American culture and belief that I've read. Told from the perspective of a particularly insightful non</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16113937865334858239">This book turned me over completely to Neil Gaiman and made me drooly and ga-ga for his writing like a Twilight fangirl on too much fairy dust. I've read a few of Gaiman's works before (<a title="Preludes and Nocturnes (The Sandman, #1) by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6657541.Preludes_and_Nocturnes__The_Sandman___1_">The Sandman Vol. 1: Preludes and Nocturnes</a>, <a title="Neverwhere by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14497.Neverwhere">Neverwhere</a>, <a title="Anansi Boys by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2744.Anansi_Boys">Anansi Boys</a>, and <a title="Stardust by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16793.Stardust">Stardust</a>), but none have blown me so completely out of the boiling seas like American Gods. <br /><br />American Gods is one of the quirkiest books on American culture and belief that I've read. Told from the perspective of a particularly insightful non-American, American Gods takes a long, hard look at how cultures and religions mesh, change, and fade in the states. How do Americans define and create faith? What gods does a godless nation--or maybe an excessively god-full nation--have with not even 300 years of national existence under its belt? With ghosts, murders, gods (both forgotten and barely worshipped), Naiman weaves a tale that suspends previous conceptions of the American psyche, turns it up on its head, and makes you question, Who really <em>is</em> that homeless crazy in the subway station calling himself Mr. Wednesday?<br /><br />Like the questions it poses, the story is not straightforward. Rather, it's a meandering road trip that takes numerous pit stops at creepy carnivals and random road-side attractions. Only, you have old worn-out gods and cranky demons for company. <br /><br />Highly recommended, and definitely one of my favorite novels of all time! </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16113937865334858239">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_263648371">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/263648371" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5253785-lyn" class="left imgcol" title="Lyn"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1428721461p2/5253785.jpg" alt="Lyn" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/263648371?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 19, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5253785-lyn" class="user" title="Lyn">Lyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4407.American_Gods" title="Paperback 9780747263746" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer263648371">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7315174606395588436">American Gods by Neil Gaiman, by the author’s own description, is a work that has inspired strong emotions and little in between – readers have either liked it a lot, or loathed it entirely. <br /><br />Reading some of the reviews bears witness to this dichotomy. I liked it, liked it a lot, but I can also understand why someone may dislike the work. Gaiman, in his storyteller way, has stepped over boundaries and stepped on toes. And not just religious or theological ideas, but nationalistic ideals as well.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7315174606395588436">American Gods by Neil Gaiman, by the author’s own description, is a work that has inspired strong emotions and little in between – readers have either liked it a lot, or loathed it entirely. <br /><br />Reading some of the reviews bears witness to this dichotomy. I liked it, liked it a lot, but I can also understand why someone may dislike the work. Gaiman, in his storyteller way, has stepped over boundaries and stepped on toes. And not just religious or theological ideas, but nationalistic ideals as well. Gaiman has painted a portrait of America that is not photographic, but impressionist enough to grasp a resemblance of us as maybe we are, and maybe he gets closer to the truth of the matter than some are comfortable with. And I’m not talking about myths, but rather, as he puts it, the myths we have lived with, tangled into the skein of our culture and even formed ourselves. <br /><br />Like many great works, and I do count this work among that group, the story works on multiple levels. It is on its surface a fantasy, rich in detail and fun to read, but also on the level of metaphor with complicated ideas and symbols thrown in, a novel that leaves the reader satisfied but still with a lot to consider once the book is put down. And like almost all great works, putting the book down is not an easy thing, and difficult to admit that the story is over.<br /><br /><img width="400" height="400" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440001876i/15916333.jpg" /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7315174606395588436">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9556874728917749833"><strong>after a number of days on the #StruggleBus, i'm setting this aside.</strong><br /><br />for now, anyway.<br /><br />it seems <em>awfully</em> preoccupied with man-eating vaginas and fatal, blowjob-related vehicular mishaps and post-mortem conjugal visitation. <br /><br />by a lady zombie. <br /><br />in other words, stuff het teenaged boys find super-exciting? <br /><br />...not so much for an adult homosexual at war with a malignant narcissist with horrendous taste in gifs and/or his own soul-sucking holiday despair.</span>

          </span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer52558202">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5216064608009120579">High 3.5 <em>maybe</em> 4 stars?<br />I can't say this is one I would recommend to everyone, and I certainly won't be shoving it down people's throats. <br />But I liked it.<br />Now, somehow I ended up with the <em>extended</em> 10 year anniversary edition. So, maybe that's why it took me forever to finish this. But I don't think that was entirely the issue. It's just a <em>loooong</em> fuckin' book. And not much happens in it action-wise, so you're not exactly flipping the pages with any intensity.<br />There's not even really (in my opinion)</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5216064608009120579">High 3.5 <em>maybe</em> 4 stars?<br />I can't say this is one I would recommend to everyone, and I certainly won't be shoving it down people's throats. <br />But I liked it.<br />Now, somehow I ended up with the <em>extended</em> 10 year anniversary edition. So, maybe that's why it took me forever to finish this. But I don't think that was entirely the issue. It's just a <em>loooong</em> fuckin' book. And not much happens in it action-wise, so you're not exactly flipping the pages with any intensity.<br />There's not even really (in my opinion) a slow-build up to anything super-exciting. And what I mean by <em>that</em>, is that I never once thought to myself, <em>Oh! Something GOOD is gonna happen in the next few pages!</em>, you know?<br />Thing is, it's got everything I could want in a book. Half-crazy gods, zombie ex wife, tarnished-but-decent hero, missing kids, and unlikely friendships. <br />However, it's also got everything I usually despise in a book. Trippy/hallucinogenic dream sequences, random quotes from other pieces of literature, plodding storyline, and no action.<br />But Neil Gaiman just oozes so much talent that somehow I still liked it. <br />Which is saying a lot, because I'm normally a real asshole when I feel like a book needed to be chopped down by about 400 pages.<br /><br />Although, unless someone can tell me that <a title="Anansi Boys by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2744.Anansi_Boys">Anansi Boys</a> is an Awesome-Not-To-Be-Missed-Roller-Coaster-Thrill-A-Minute-Ride, I'm gonna have to say <em>no</em> to that one. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5216064608009120579">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1417156782">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1417156782" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell" class="left imgcol" title="Maxwell"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1444015697p2/1381728.jpg" alt="Maxwell" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1417156782?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 22, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell" class="user" title="Maxwell">Maxwell</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/13101468-american-gods" title="Paperback 9780062080233" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1381728-maxwell?shelf=i-own-it" class="actionLinkLite">i-own-it</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1381728-maxwell?shelf=2015" class="actionLinkLite">2015</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1381728-maxwell?shelf=audiobook" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1417156782">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13751165001839601699">In the preface of this edition of <em>American Gods</em> which is the Author's Preferred Text for the 10th anniversary, Neil Gaiman says that much of what is included in this version was cut for the original publication. He says it wanders more, but he wanted to include them anyway. I found that to be a poor decision. I felt that much of the book was filler, and while entertaining on its own, added very little to the core of the story. <br /><br />"What is the core of the story?" you ask. We follow a man convenientl</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13751165001839601699">In the preface of this edition of <em>American Gods</em> which is the Author's Preferred Text for the 10th anniversary, Neil Gaiman says that much of what is included in this version was cut for the original publication. He says it wanders more, but he wanted to include them anyway. I found that to be a poor decision. I felt that much of the book was filler, and while entertaining on its own, added very little to the core of the story. <br /><br />"What is the core of the story?" you ask. We follow a man conveniently named Shadow who, after getting released from jail, gets swept up in an 'adventure' (I'm skeptical to call it such because it feels more like a pilgrimage or nomadic wandering) with some American Gods. Shadow is himself a shadow of a real person: he has little personality, he follows blindly much of what he is told to do, and ultimately he doesn't really show up as a character until the stage lights are blinding on him and he has no other option but to perform. He's a likeable enough guy, but not memorable. I did enjoy his interaction with other people though, and there were certain parts where I pitied the guy, but never fully empathized.<br /><br />The story, like I mentioned above, wanders. It takes you all across America, to its forgotten places. But the structure of the story wanders as well. There are interludes and 'Coming to America' chapters--they tell one-off stories of the different ways immigrants bring their gods to America, and there are random conversations thrust in here or there that are meant to bring a greater scope to the story. But in the end it's a pretty straightforward story of Shadow and his involvement with the gods.<br /><br />My main complaint was really the length of the novel. There were, in my opinion, many unnecessary or at least unnecessarily long portions that could've been cut (and probably were in the original). I was a bit frustrated in the middle because, while Shadow is learning everything as he goes, so are you. And that doesn't lend itself to a very forward-moving plot. You are just as confused by and unsure of what is going as Shadow himself.<br /><br />I've come to realize I'm more a fan of Gaiman's middle grade &amp; children's books. When he fully commits to fantasy, I enjoy it much more. But the blending of fantasy with a sort of didactic analysis of American culture didn't really work for me in this instance. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13751165001839601699">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_227515946">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/227515946" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3642045-sue" class="left imgcol" title="Sue"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" alt="Sue" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/227515946?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 25, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3642045-sue" class="user" title="Sue">Sue</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/11823620-american-gods" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=myth-legend-fairy-tale" class="actionLinkLite">myth-legend-fairy-tale</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=kindle" class="actionLinkLite">kindle</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2014" class="actionLinkLite">read-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer227515946">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer638816162892662908">An incredible adventure!<br /><br />The American Gods/gods, like the many peoples they accompanied, arrived on this continent over the past hundreds, thousands of years. And like average people, these gods assimilated in ways large and small into their environment, some losing parts of themselves, some holding on to traces of past glory in different ways and forms. As the people assimilated their beliefs often weakened too affecting their old gods. <br /><br />But change has come. Major change in the form of new belief</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText638816162892662908">An incredible adventure!<br /><br />The American Gods/gods, like the many peoples they accompanied, arrived on this continent over the past hundreds, thousands of years. And like average people, these gods assimilated in ways large and small into their environment, some losing parts of themselves, some holding on to traces of past glory in different ways and forms. As the people assimilated their beliefs often weakened too affecting their old gods. <br /><br />But change has come. Major change in the form of new beliefs, new gods, large and small, which threaten the old gods existence. And Neil Gaiman has brought us into the center of the fray, old vs new. <br /><br />In what will be the first of many dream-like experiences, Shadow, central to the story and to my enjoyment, experiences the following:<br /><br /><br /><em>Shadow was in a dark place, and the thing staring at<br /> him wore a buffalo's head, rank and furry with huge wet<br /> eyes. Its body was a man's body, oiled and slick.<br /> "Changes are coming," said the buffalo without moving<br /> his lips. "There are certain decisions that will have to<br /> be made."<br /> Firelight flickered from wet cave walls.<br /> "Where am I?" Shadow asked.<br /> "In the earth and under the earth," said the buffalo<br /> man. "You are where the forgotten wait." His eyes were <br /> liquid black marbles, and his voice was a rumble from<br /> beneath the world. He smelled like wet cow. "Believe,"<br /> said the rumbling voice. "If you are to survive, you<br /> must believe."<br /> "Believe what?" asked Shadow. "What should I believe?"<br /> He stared at Shadow, the buffalo man, and he drew<br /> himself up huge, and his eyes filled with fire. He <br /> opened his spit-flecked buffalo mouth and it was red <br /> inside with the flames that burned inside him, under<br /> the earth.<br /> "Everything," roared the buffalo man.</em><br /> (loc 450)<br /><br /><br />The writing is so wonderful...Gaiman so obviously loves his task. Here Shadow is learning more about the world of the gods.<br /><br /><br /><em>the voice spoke once more, as if it were addressing<br /> a class, saying, "These are the gods who have passed out <br /> of memory. Even their names are lost. The people who<br /> worshiped them are as forgotten as their gods. Their totems<br /> are long since broken and cast down. Their last priests <br /> died without passing on their secrets.<br /> "Gods die. And when they truly die they are unmourned<br /> and unremembered. Ideas are more difficult to kill than <br /> people, but they can be killed, in the end."</em><br /> (loc 1176)<br /><br /><br />Then there are short sentences that stopped me: "Chicago happened slowly like a migraine." (1417)<br /><br />And the essence of it:<br /><br /><br /><em>"When the people came to America they brought us with <br /> them. They brought me and Loki and Thor, Anansi and the<br /> Lion-God, Leprechauns and Cluracans and Banshees, Kubera <br /> and Frau Holle and Ashtaroth, and they brought you. We <br /> rode here in their minds, and we took root. We traveled<br /> with the settlers to the new lands across the ocean.<br /> "The land is vast. Soon enough, our people abandoned<br /> us, remembered us only as creatures of the old land, as<br /> things that had not come with them to the new. Our true<br /> believers passed on, or stopped believing, and we were <br /> left, lost and scared and dispossessed, to get by on what<br /> little smidgens of worship or belief we could find...</em><br /> (loc 2489)<br /><br /><br />There is so much here for an interested reader, much more than I can possibly tell. If you have an interest in myths, fables, old religions and new story telling, this book may well be for you. I know it is not for everyone but those who love it, do love it. It did win me from a position of like to admire to love by the end. Thank you Mr Gaiman.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="638816162892662908">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1032101913?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 17, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/29931457-franco-santos" class="user" title="Franco  Santos">Franco  Santos</a>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1032101913">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5792640654251504510">Un libro como sólo Neil Gaiman sabe escribir. Tiene esa fantasía tan peculiar del autor, en su expresión más fina. <strong>Una combinación de magia divina con la característica tétrica y umbría de este gran escritor.</strong><br /><br /><img width="400" height="200" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1424483144i/13767656._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Es una de las novelas más extrañas y hasta bizarras que leí.<strong> Tiene partes que son, en mi opinión, graciosas de lo ridículo de su desarrollo</strong>. Otras me resultaron un poco pesadas, como los interludios. Éstos a muchos les encantó; a mí me aburrieron todos. Otro aspecto negativo que tengo que re</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5792640654251504510">Un libro como sólo Neil Gaiman sabe escribir. Tiene esa fantasía tan peculiar del autor, en su expresión más fina. <strong>Una combinación de magia divina con la característica tétrica y umbría de este gran escritor.</strong><br /><br /><img width="400" height="200" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1424483144i/13767656._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Es una de las novelas más extrañas y hasta bizarras que leí.<strong> Tiene partes que son, en mi opinión, graciosas de lo ridículo de su desarrollo</strong>. Otras me resultaron un poco pesadas, como los interludios. Éstos a muchos les encantó; a mí me aburrieron todos. Otro aspecto negativo que tengo que resaltar es que no sabés qué estás leyendo hasta pasada la primera mitad del relato.<br /><br /><strong>Por lo demás, excelsitud</strong>. Me resultó muy adictivo; tanto es así que en dos días ya lo tenía terminado. Me tengo que mostrar ambivalente con el personaje principal, Sombra. En algunas partes me agradó y en otras no tanto. <br /><br />Creo que es un libro que se podría disfrutar muchísimo más si conocieras los dioses tratados en la trama. <strong>No obstante, se disfruta lo suficiente y no causa ningún problema más que ese.</strong><br /><br />El final es formidable. No me lo esperaba. Es uno de los momentos más impactantes de la novela. <strong>Neil trató el punto álgido del relato con soberbia, creando un clima absorbente.</strong> Muy bueno.<br /><br /><strong>En fin, excelente trabajo de Gaiman</strong>. Es ameno, fluido y entretenido. Muy original, también.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer976628915">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2321115614202606291">Only Neil Gaiman could get a FULL NARRATION CAST. O.O Does that ever happen in life? I’ve never seen it done except in this case. But wow, this is one of the best audiobooks I’ve ever listened to. Each character has its own narrator and Gaiman himself narrators the Coming to American sections (which seemed completely random, but, whatever, Neil Gaiman’s voice tho).<br /><br />I hadn’t read the book before listening so, I was a little taken back at how Shadow seemed to be a really apathetic protagonist. Oh,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2321115614202606291">Only Neil Gaiman could get a FULL NARRATION CAST. O.O Does that ever happen in life? I’ve never seen it done except in this case. But wow, this is one of the best audiobooks I’ve ever listened to. Each character has its own narrator and Gaiman himself narrators the Coming to American sections (which seemed completely random, but, whatever, Neil Gaiman’s voice tho).<br /><br />I hadn’t read the book before listening so, I was a little taken back at how Shadow seemed to be a really apathetic protagonist. Oh, his wife just died the day before he’s due to get out of jail? He’s not bothered. Someone wants to kill him? He’s not bothered. Weird shit is afoot? He’s really, really not bothered. I didn’t get that about him and would have loved to see more emotion from him. To be fair, he does open up later near the end, and I guess that’s the point (kind of?), but that’s my gripe.<br /><br />I was really impressed by the plotting and ending. Wouldn’t it be cool to crawl up into Neil Gaiman’s head?</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('l17npdLxOPZz+nuBmb+Obakk28zoYEKuaRCkEEVqMZfyith2ICMsDP8iDzrn3mjf4NMY1aG9BQQDGaGCif/H0g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/trivia/work/1970226-american-gods">Trivia About American Gods</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
        <div class="mediumText">
          90 trivia questions 
          <br />

        </div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1970226-american-gods">Quotes from American Gods</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“I can believe things that are true and things that aren't true and I can believe things where nobody knows if they're true or not. <br /><br />I can believe in Santa Claus and the Easter Bunny and the Beatles and Marilyn Monroe and Elvis and Mister Ed. Listen - I believe that people are perfectable, that knowledge is infinite, that the world is run by secret banking cartels and is visited by aliens on a regular basis, nice ones that look like wrinkled lemurs and bad ones who mutilate cattle and want our water and our women. <br /><br />I believe that the future sucks and I believe that the future rocks and I believe that one day White Buffalo Woman is going to come back and kick everyone's ass. I believe that all men are just overgrown boys with deep problems communicating and that the decline in good sex in America is coincident with the decline in drive-in movie theaters from state to state. <br /><br />I believe that all politicians are unprincipled crooks and I still believe that they are better than the alternative. I believe that California is going to sink into the sea when the big one comes, while Florida is going to dissolve into madness and alligators and toxic waste. <br /><br />I believe that antibacterial soap is destroying our resistance to dirt and disease so that one day we'll all be wiped out by the common cold like martians in War of the Worlds. <br /><br />I believe that the greatest poets of the last century were Edith Sitwell and Don Marquis, that jade is dried dragon sperm, and that thousands of years ago in a former life I was a one-armed Siberian shaman. <br /><br />I believe that mankind's destiny lies in the stars. I believe that candy really did taste better when I was a kid, that it's aerodynamically impossible for a bumble bee to fly, that light is a wave and a particle, that there's a cat in a box somewhere who's alive and dead at the same time (although if they don't ever open the box to feed it it'll eventually just be two different kinds of dead), and that there are stars in the universe billions of years older than the universe itself. <br /><br />I believe in a personal god who cares about me and worries and oversees everything I do. I believe in an impersonal god who set the universe in motion and went off to hang with her girlfriends and doesn't even know that I'm alive. I believe in an empty and godless universe of causal chaos, background noise, and sheer blind luck. <br /><br />I believe that anyone who says sex is overrated just hasn't done it properly. I believe that anyone who claims to know what's going on will lie about the little things too. <br /><br />I believe in absolute honesty and sensible social lies. I believe in a woman's right to choose, a baby's right to live, that while all human life is sacred there's nothing wrong with the death penalty if you can trust the legal system implicitly, and that no one but a moron would ever trust the legal system. <br /><br />I believe that life is a game, that life is a cruel joke, and that life is what happens when you're alive and that you might as well lie back and enjoy it.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/4986-i-can-believe-things-that-are-true-and-things-that" class="actionLinkLite">7596 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“What I say is, a town isn’t a town without a bookstore. It may call itself a town, but unless it’s got a bookstore, it knows it’s not foolin’ a soul.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/53787-what-i-say-is-a-town-isn-t-a-town-without" class="actionLinkLite">4117 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a href="/work/quotes/1970226-american-gods" style="float: right" class="actionLink">More quotes…</a>
